                        "subject": "<md>Could be a few reasons. `std::array` is fixed size while `std::vector` is dynamic sized. If they wanted to pass in `array` where the size is unknown, you would have to do some weird templating stuff. Also in languages such as JavaScript and Python, all lists are implemented as dynamic arrays, so perhaps they wanted to make sure solving a Leetcode problem in C++ was analogous to solving it in those other languages.\n\nThere's nothing wrong with raw arrays, but they can be tricky to work with especially when trying to solve Leetcode problems since you are dealing with raw pointers.\n\nIf you use `std::array` or `std::vector`, you can use iterators and do arithmetic with them just like you would with pointers!</md>",

                        "content": "<md>Partially because our grading scheme is a little bit new (with the move to curving only exams) and we've relied on putting conservative numbers in the syllabus. We also started using it during the pandemic, which has brought some uncertainty and to a certain degree, we've relaxed the grade thresholds a bit more than in the past. Part of this is to account for disruption due to the pandemic in all students lives, and part of it is to account for anomalies in the grading distribution that are hard to quantify (e.g. students are allowed to withdraw until the last day of classes, which skews the distribution) and we'd rather be a bit conservative. This applied for the pass/fail thresholds and also for the fact that you'll see a higher average GPA and e.g. proportion of As than in past EECS 280 grade distributions. Sometime after we get final grades submitted, I'll write up a bit more about our grading scheme and the distribution and we'll put out a Canvas announcement (we generally publish some information about the grade distribution at the end of each term).\n\nIn the future, I think we may end up moving toward a system where we don't change them at all from what is published (unless some unexpected disruption occurs during the term) or at least not very much at all. I think the 70% exam threshold ended up stressing out a number of students who may not have needed to worry if the actual 66% threshold was given up front.</md>"

                    {
                        "anon": "no",
                        "folders": [],
                        "data": null,
                        "subject": "<md>From Prof Juett.\n\n> I think someone could make a reasonable argument that because the exams scores (those are curved scores, so they'd be something like 40% raw) are fairly low, a student with those scores shouldn't pass the course. Absent of whether that's correct, I think the argument _could_ and _would_ be made or at least considered. The project scores are high - perhaps this would suggest it's too easy to get a high score on the project (I don't think that's the case in 280, but let's pretend). That might put pressure on to somehow make the projects harder to finish, to put in some sneaky test cases, have stricter grading, etc. It also might put pressure that the threshold for a C should be higher. EECS 280 uses 70% as the cutoff for C, rather than a traditional 72% or 73%. If there is a concern that the specific student in question (who did well on projects but not on exams) has not demonstrated they're able to succeed in all areas of the course, this puts pressure on the overall percentage in the class (a single, scalar measure) to have stricter grading. I don't think either of those are good pressures, and the alternate grading mechanisms help relieve them. For projects, the pressures to make the projects harder or add arbitrary sneaky private test cases to try to force a distribution are quieted because we can set a specific project threshold to something reasonable. For overall scores, we can make it a bit easier to get a C overall without the pressure to somehow use overall grading as a proxy to account for low exam grades. So, I know it's easy enough to think about the alternate minimums as another way to keep students from earning a C, but many years ago at the time they were implemented we actually also made it _easier_ to earn a C via overall percentage as a direct consequence. In general, it's a more progressive grading system that allows us to more finely express what we feel is important in grades as an evaluative measure. (edited)</md>",

                "subject": "While you're waiting for grades, a note on the curve...",
                "created": "2022-04-25T19:48:44Z",
                "content": "<p>We're getting a few private questions here about the curve, so I thought I'd just make a public note since others may be wondering as well.</p>\n<p><br />It might be easiest to explain with a hypothetical curve. DISCLAIMER - this is NOT the real curve (but I have a feeling the curve for the final exam will be\u00a0at least this generous, because it seems like it was a difficult exam).<br /><br />Let's say your individual exam works out to have a computed mean of 75 and std dev of 15. Let's also say the faculty make the decision to curve the final to a target mean of 82 and std dev of 9 (that's what we did for the midterm, the final will probably be similar).</p>\n<p></p>\n<p>We curve using z-scores to adjust the distribution. Say your raw score is R. Then you can compute your curved score C by:<br /><br />Z = (R - 75) / 15<br />C = 82 + Z * 9<br /><br />Your final score is max(R, C). Here's a plot, raw score on x-axis and final score on y-axis.<br /><br /></p>\n<p><img src=\"/redirect/s3?bucket=uploads&amp;prefix=paste%2Fh6sd65s7shv5mv%2F875ec406477f74aa6001f650ed9432b18701e25d022734b60f93e2c1ef7891bd%2Fimage.png\" alt=\"image.png\" width=\"278\" height=\"196\" /></p>\n<p></p>\n<p>Most people will see an increase in their score based on the curve, given that raw scores are lower on average (~75) than the score we curve to (~82). I put the crosshairs on that plot to show the point at which the raw score would switch over to being better than the curved score, which is around a raw score of ~93.<br /><br />So, even if you score near the average or the target average, you're still getting a bump from the curve. At the end of the day, the curve is to adjust for the difficulty of the exam you got - not so much to adjust for how you did on the exam. Nearly everyone I've talked to thinks the final exam was more difficult than the midterm exam (which is not surprising - the midterm exam had unusually high raw scores, and retrospectively the questions there happened to be easier than we usually put on an exam).</p>"
